3. Statements by the President

脗聽脗聽President. 芒聙聯 First of all, as we mark 35 years since German reunification, we are joined today by the President of the German Bundestag, Julia Kl脙露ckner, who is with us in the diplomatic gallery. Thank you for being with us.

(Applause)

The reunification of Germany was a defining moment in European history that symbolised hope, transformation and the enduring power of freedom. It showed what people determined to live in democracy can achieve, and what politics, diplomacy and courage can deliver. That moment paved the way for a stronger, more united Europe, and it is that spirit of 1990 that we need to recapture as we face the challenges of today.

Liebe Julia, sehr geehrte Frau Pr脙陇sidentin, herzlich willkommen!

Dear colleagues, over the last weeks, the world marked three years since the killing of 22芒聙聭year芒聙聭old Jina Mahsa Amini at the hands of the Iranian police. Her death sparked the 'Women, Life, Freedom' movement honoured by this Parliament with the 2023 Sakharov Prize.

Jina Mahsa Amini's life and legacy live on in the spirit of all those who stood up over the last years, and in all those who are still pushing for liberty in Iran. I am proud that this House has stood on the right side of history, and that the brave women and men standing up to repression know that they are not alone.

Dear colleagues, last month we all witnessed the shocking assassination of Charlie Kirk during an event on an American University campus. He was 31脗聽years old and was known to anyone with a social media account. This senseless act of murder should be treated with dignity, and I would urge everyone to avoid political theatre or cheap point芒聙聭scoring.

Let me also say that this assassination underscores the need for all of us in public life to do what we can to dial down the divisive rhetoric and demonisation aimed at political opponents. We can all do better. Violence has no place in our society. Words and ideas, not weapons, and not hate, must shape our dialogue. We have seen it time and again across the world with horrific consequences. We saw it in Utah last month. We saw it in Minnesota in June with Melissa and Mark Hortman. We are not immune to it in Europe either. That is not the way. Political violence can have no room in our societies and communities.

Dear colleagues, we were reminded only last week of how violence and hatred can reach into our own communities, with the deadly attack on a synagogue in Manchester on Yom Kippur, the holiest day of the Jewish calendar. I know that our thoughts are with the families and loved ones of the victims and the wider community impacted in Manchester. This House stands proudly against anti-Semitism, against all forms of hatred and extremism, and in solidarity with the people of the United Kingdom.

Tomorrow marks two years since the horrific 7脗聽October terrorist attacks in Israel by Hamas that saw 1脗聽200 people killed and 251 people taken hostage, many of whom remain in the tunnels of Gaza. It is a day that will live forever in infamy in the history of our time.脗聽The attack ignited a cycle of war and violence that has claimed the lives of tens of thousands, with the situation on the ground remaining critical.

So much has been destroyed. So many now know hunger and war. For all those mothers waiting for 730 days for any news of the fate of their children, the concept of time has become another burden for them to carry.

There is a real and comprehensive peace plan now on the table that provides a framework towards peace, stability and reconstruction in Gaza, that could finally see the return home of all those hostages still in Hamas tunnels, that keeps a two芒聙聭state solution alive, and that eases the suffering we see. I know that we will debate this later this session, but it is a pivotal moment that can finally bring about the end to the intergenerational cycle of bloodshed, terror and violence. There is a clear alternative to perpetual war, and that alternative must be seized.

So, dear colleagues, as we remember all those lost, I invite you to join me in observing a moment of silence.

脗聽脗聽Ursula von der Leyen, President of the Commission. 芒聙聯 Madam President, honourable Members, the world is in the most precarious and perilous state it has been in for decades, and Europe is on high alert. From reckless airspace incursions to attempts at economic coercions. From ruthless attacks on Ukraine to direct threats to our own security. Europe is threatened from the East. It is challenged from within. And for the Global South, what we do next is being scrutinised closely.

As we saw at the recent United Nations General Assembly, the existing international order is threatened by splintering faster than we expected. The point I'm making here is that we are in a period of maximum uncertainty and explosive volatility. This chaotic and threatening landscape was the reason I started my State of the Union address by saying, 'Europe is in a fight'. And it is why, on that day, I made a pitch for unity.

Today, I want to reaffirm my commitment to work with all of you to keep that unity 芒聙聯 inside this Parliament, among Member States and between our institutions. The point of this unity is not necessarily for all of us to agree on every detail. In fact, tension and debate is an inherent and important part of policy-making. But 芒聙聯 perhaps more than ever before 芒聙聯 it is about the bigger picture, about rallying around the things we do have in common to deliver for the Europeans in this dangerous world: to bolster our security, to protect our democracy, to support our industry, to strengthen our social model. The point I am making is that we need to focus on what really matters, which is to deliver for Europeans.

Because, honourable Members, the truth is that our adversaries are not only ready to exploit any divisions, they are actively inciting those divisions in the first place. Just look at the last few weeks. On the same day as we met here to discuss the State of the Union, two dozen Russian drones entered the Polish airspace. We've seen Russian fighter jets pushing boundaries above Estonian airspace, and we have seen a number of drones in the skies above Europe causing disruption at our airports.

Look at what happened in Sochi just last week, where Putin gave his annual Valdai Club speech. We should listen very carefully to his words. He put the blame on Europe for his continued war of aggression in Ukraine. He crowed about what he coined as 'the cracks of the edifice of Europe' and the 'shaken unity' within our Union. He is not hiding his contempt for our Union and the foundations it is built on, and he is not hiding his glee and support for all of his obedient friends in Europe who are doing his work for him. This is the oldest trick in the book. Sow division, spread disinformation, create a scapegoat. All to turn Europeans against each other, to try to lower our guard while we fight each other, to weaken our resolve and our resilience. This is a trap, and we simply cannot fall for it.

The strongest message we can send instead is that of unity. Whether we are working on our defence readiness, on building our democracy shield, on boosting our competitiveness or on creating quality jobs for all in today's modern economy, this is what we need to focus on. And this is what myself and the entire College here by my side want to work on with this House.

Honourable Members, of course I know there are some of you who are still unsure how to vote later this week, but who are also concerned about some of the issues these motions mention in passing. Whether on Gaza or Ukraine, trade or the United States, I know that they come from a place of genuine and legitimate concerns. 脗聽脗聽Glenn Micallef, Member of the Commission. 芒聙聯 Dear President, honourable Members, the European Sport Model as we know it, with its values and with its features, is today under attack.

The night the Super League was presented in April 2021 was for many an abrupt wake芒聙聭up call. Milan, Madrid, Berlin, Cologne and Kaunas 芒聙聯 these were some of the cities where basketball Euroleague finals were played in the last 10脗聽years; all of them European capitals and European cities; cities with a long tradition in basketball and in sport. This year, clubs from France, Greece and T脙录rkiye contested the most芒聙聭wanted title of European basketball in Abu Dhabi. And as from this season, a basketball club from Dubai has been invited to take part in this European tournament. Does this make sense from a sporting merit perspective? And what about the fans of these clubs?

The decision to take the Euroleague Final Four to Abu Dhabi, or to play Italian or Spanish domestic football league games in Australia or the United States, are choices that touch the heart of European sport. European sport belongs to the communities, the arenas and the fans that built these clubs and these competitions. Taking it away from them, many kilometres away from Europe, changes the very nature of those events, raising questions of access and belonging.

Let me be very clear: there is nothing wrong with seeking to grow sport. Basketball, football, water polo or other sports all have a global appeal. The point is that growth must not come at the expense of those who make sport attractive 芒聙聯 the fans, the players and the communities. This is why the European Sport Model is under attack, and it is also why it is in need of renewal.

The own initiative report on the role of EU policies in shaping the European Sport Model that we are discussing today is a sign of renewal and it is a good building block. I want to thank the rapporteur, the honourable Bogdan Zdrojewski, and the shadow rapporteurs, for their work. Work that sends a very strong signal; a signal that the European Parliament and the European Commission have a shared vision for sport in Europe. As the report outlines, together we will strive to ensure that sport serves and promotes the interests of all, rather than the profits of the few. This goal is fundamental to sport's role as a public good, and to seizing its full contribution to strengthening Europe's solidarity, cohesion and competitiveness.

But sport and physical activity also play a key role in promoting democracy, human rights, peace, respect, solidarity, diversity and equality. They are essential tools in the fight against non-communicable diseases such as diabetes, cardiovascular illness, obesity and depression. Because one of the greatest pandemics of our time is not a virus, but a silent crisis of mental health. And it is our young people who are mostly being affected by this. Just to give an example, data reveals that 50脗聽% of young Europeans, half our youth, report unmet needs when it comes to mental health. And the 2023 Flash Eurobarometer on Mental Health shows that 59脗聽% of people aged between 16 and 24 had an emotional or psychosocial problem, such as feeling depressed or anxious in the previous 12脗聽months. And regular physical activity is one of the most effective and cost efficient forms of prevention that we have. Sports can save lives, and if we succeed in keeping our citizens active, we reduce healthcare costs, improve productivity, and most importantly, improve our quality of life. This dimension of sport as part of health policy will remain at the heart of the European Sport Model.

I am also pleased to see the emphasis that you place on the worrying decline in volunteering at the grassroots and the need to secure athletes rights and wellbeing. Similarly, safeguarding good governance is vital to ensuring the equal representation of stakeholders. This means we need to include athletes and fans in decision making whilst respecting the territoriality of European sport. And in parallel, we urgently need to step up our work to combat the piracy of live sport events. This is crucial to the financial sustainability of the sector, because financial integrity and competitive balance are 'must haves' for a thriving European sports sector that can deliver for athletes and fans alike at all levels.

To address these priorities and to answer to one of your calls in the report, the Commission is developing a strategy on the European Sport Model. And let me assure you that the own initiative report you are presenting for adoption today will be an important contribution. The views of the European Parliament will be taken into account.

Let me also take this opportunity to touch upon the next MFF. I am pleased that our proposal for an increased Erasmus+ budget matches our ambition to promote and strengthen the European Sport Model and European sport, and synergies with other funds will be necessary. Public support, financial and political for sport also is essential. At the same time, our financial support should never compromise the autonomy of sports and federations. Sports organisations must be able to take decisions, and take responsibility, and govern sport independently.

Nevertheless, and I will conclude on this point, on the wider context, sport does not exist in isolation from geopolitics or from our values. Sport, with its universal language of peace and fair play, must never be instrumental to deepen divides. Sports should remain a bridge between people, a tool for dialogue, and a reminder that even in times of conflict, respect and solidarity must prevail. We count on your continued support to realise this shared vision of sport.

Member States should ratify the Macolin Convention. The Commission should act against illegal streaming of live content that deprives the ecosystem of deserved income.

Yes, there are new and evolving trends in ownership and we need to be very careful with them.

Yes, domestic competition matches should not be played abroad. I do not think we should invent a definition for 'domestic' or 'international' in order to understand what should be played where. Wherefore the opposition of today is a good day for governance in sport.

And yes, European sport does not recognise breakaway competitions. Full stop. Otherwise, we should call it a day.

脗聽脗聽Nina Carberry (PPE). 芒聙聯 Mr脗聽President, I would like to thank the rapporteur for his report.

I recently held an event in Parliament highlighting the barriers to physical activity and the opportunities we have to break them down. What was clear is that investment across the EU remains insufficient. We know the benefits of participation in physical activity, from reducing chronic diseases, improving mental well-being, fostering inclusion to supporting people with disabilities.

I've seen these benefits first-hand during my sporting career, where health and fitness were central to the job. When I retired from sport, I quickly felt how inactivity can creep up on us and the challenges that come with keeping fit. We must keep campaigning to encourage physical activity and participation in sport for all, and this needs to be backed up with momentum and resources from Europe.

As an MEP, I've been lucky enough to visit many counties across my constituency this year to see first-hand the great sporting initiatives taking place. At Mullaghmore Equestrian Centre in Monaghan, therapeutic horse riding helps children with autism and disabilities build confidence and improve their physical strength. Meanwhile, in Galway, the installation of 'BoxUps' in public parks has provided free access to sports equipment, helping communities come together and encouraging participation for all.

Sport breaks down barriers and can truly change lives, so why aren't we investing more? Commissioner, in the next long-term EU budget we have a real opportunity to recognise sports infrastructure as an investment area within the European Regional Development Fund framework and to ringfence Erasmus+ funding for sport. Now, more than ever, we must start treating physical activity as an essential part of preventative healthcare, before the cost of inaction is too great.

脗聽脗聽Glenn Micallef, Member of the Commission. 芒聙聯 Mr President, dear Members, I want to thank you all for your support and for this constructive discussion that we had this evening. Most of you mentioned some of the challenges that we face, but the Commission and the European Parliament must work together closely to seize the full potential of sport for our communities, but also to promote our shared values embodied in our European sport model. This is key to reap sports' full contribution to build cohesive, fair and healthy societies.

Every part of our community has a role in achieving this shared vision 芒聙聯 a vision that is based on values that are enshrined in our Treaties, and that we stand for every day. In this I count on the support of the honourable Members of this House to translate this vision into concrete action benefiting our societies and the citizens of Europe.

脗聽脗聽Rihards Kols (ECR), in writing. 芒聙聯 The European Sport Model must reflect our core values: inclusion, dignity, and equal opportunity. That means giving full and proper recognition to Special Olympics and athletes with intellectual disabilities芒聙聰who are too often sidelined in the broader policy conversation.

Inclusion through sport is not charity. It is a matter of human rights, societal resilience, and community strength. The Special Olympics movement is a clear example of how sport can transform lives芒聙聰building confidence, skills, and belonging. But for this to be sustainable, it must be structurally integrated into the European Sport Model.

EU policies play a decisive role here. Funding streams, visibility in strategic documents, and access to infrastructure must not be limited to elite or professional sport alone. We must ensure that the grassroots, inclusive, and transformative work of Special Olympics is not only supported but recognized as a key pillar of European sport.

This is not a side initiative芒聙聰it is central to the purpose of sport in Europe. A truly inclusive Sport Model does not just allow space for Special Olympics; it sees it as essential. The EU must lead in setting that standard芒聙聰and back it with concrete, consistent policy support.

脗聽

16. Intergenerational fairness in Europe on the occasion of the International Day of Older Persons (debate)
Video of the speeches
脗聽

脗聽脗聽President. 芒聙聯 The next item is the Commission statement on intergenerational fairness in Europe on the occasion of the International Day of Older Persons (2025/2902(RSP)).

脗聽
脗聽脗聽
脗聽

脗聽脗聽Glenn Micallef, Member of the Commission. 芒聙聯 Mr脗聽President, honourable Members, thank you for today's plenary debate on intergenerational fairness in Europe.

As we have this debate on the occasion of the International Day of Older Persons, let me start by saying that in 2024, in the European Union, life expectancy at birth is estimated at 81.7 years of age. This is the highest it has ever been and this is something we have to celebrate. It's a testament to medical advancements, high living standards and progress in public health across the European Union.

But it also means that today we enjoy longer and healthier lives, and that we need to start nurturing the longevity ahead when we are young. It is a recognition that the generational perspective is not static. The young of today are the old of tomorrow and intergenerational fairness is about the cycle of life that we share. It is about ensuring that when today's young become older, they inherit a society that cares for them and that they in turn can pass on something stronger to those who follow.

Longevity is a new demographic trend and in adapting to it, we need to fight stereotypes, we need to fight discrimination and we need to create an environment that enables people of all ages to realise their ambitions.

The results of the 2023 Eurobarometer report on discrimination in the European Union indicate that 45脗聽% of Europeans 芒聙聯 almost half 芒聙聯 believe that age discrimination is widespread in Europe, and 52脗聽% of Europeans mention age as the main criteria that may put a candidate at a disadvantage in a recruitment process.

So, to allow all citizens to thrive and make the most of their opportunities, and in order to build a just, fit芒聙聭for芒聙聭the芒聙聭future and cohesive society, we need to put age equality at the centre of our policies.脗聽We need to strengthen the communication and cooperation between generations, and we need to adapt to new demographic trends that protect the EU's long芒聙聭term economic prosperity and competitiveness.

When we speak about breaking stereotypes, it means challenging the ideas that older people care only about pensions and that younger people care only about climate change, for example. This is already happening and we need to build on the best practices that we have.

Take, for example, the 'Grandparents for Climate' movement. It advocates for climate justice and highlights the responsibility of older generations to protect the environment for the future. I love this example because it shows what intergenerational fairness is really about. It means going beyond self芒聙聭interest of any single generation and working together for a better future.

It is also about preserving our unique European social model, working to sustain the welfare of older generations, but also thinking about the seniors of the future while empowering younger people. This serves all Europeans because today's taxpayers are tomorrow's pensioners.

I am working hard to contribute to creating policies fit for the future and for all generations through the strategy on intergenerational fairness, which is planned for adoption in the first half of 2026. This strategy will have a double objective: first, to ensure that our decisions today do not harm future generations, and second, to foster a renewed engagement between generations so that the young and the old in Europe can stay together and stand together.

In terms of features and processes, there are three main elements that I wish to highlight here today. First, this strategy is being designed with the help of a broad participatory process. In January, we started by consulting representatives from civil society, academia and international organisations.

Now, a European Citizens' Panel on Intergenerational Fairness is on their way. One meeting of the 150 randomly selected Europeans already took place in Brussels between 12 and 14脗聽September. I had the privilege of attending that session and meeting Europeans of all ages who will help shape this strategy. I am looking forward to the results of their deliberations and their recommendations for policymakers on intergenerational fairness. Soon, we will also launch a call for evidence where all citizens and stakeholders can have their say.

Second, the strategy is wide芒聙聭ranging in scope. It will cut across policy fields from welfare, long芒聙聭term care, housing and social mobility, to tackling climate change and strengthening Europe's sustainable competitiveness.

Third, this will require a whole芒聙聭of芒聙聭Commission and a whole芒聙聭of芒聙聭society approach. This strategy will serve as a platform for projects and a framework for policies that benefit all generations, including future ones. This could see, for example, the mainstreaming of considerations related to age equality and combating age discrimination across all Commission policies, but also strategies like the generational renewal strategy in agriculture or intergenerational housing.

I want the strategy to mark a new mindset in policymaking, one that sees intergenerational fairness and long芒聙聭term thinking considered in all of our policies. Honourable Members, I count on your support as we continue developing the strategy.脗聽Together, we can produce policy that reflects the experiences, concerns and aspirations of European citizens of all ages. Together, we can ensure that our policies are fit not only for the needs of today's citizens, but also for the well芒聙聭being of future generations. Together, we can build a legacy and a way of governing that balances the present demands with future responsibilities.

脗聽脗聽Glenn Micallef, Member of the Commission. 芒聙聯 Madam脗聽President, I'd like to thank the honourable Members for the discussion today, for your valuable and constructive points. It is actually quite clear that we have a common vision 芒聙聯 a vision where in Europe no one is discriminated against on the basis of age.

The strategy on intergenerational fairness, on which we are working right now, represents a shared commitment that we have to build a Europe that is inclusive, cohesive, resilient, fit for the future and fair across generations. This will build on what we inherited from older generations and focus on us becoming good ancestors. This will be a platform for all Commissioners to roll out projects promoting intergenerational fairness in their work.

Let me elaborate a bit on the concept of 'intergenerational fairness', our understanding of 'intergenerational fairness'. 'Fairness' means respecting the dignity and contributions of older generations in Europe. At the same time, we have to ensure that young people have opportunities to build their lives. An intergenerationally fair approach sees older citizens as contributors in society, whose experience wisdom and civic engagement are key for Europe's future. At the same time, fairness demands that the younger generations are not overburdened, that they are given the right conditions of education, housing and the democratic voice to thrive. Intergenerational fairness is therefore about creating a society where people age with dignity and where young people have the right opportunities in life.

By embedding this perspective across all of our work, we can ensure that fairness between generations becomes a standard consideration rather than an isolated initiative. I look forward to continuing this dialogue with you, and to working together to ensure that the strategy can be successfully implemented across Europe.

17. Revision of the Visa Suspension Mechanism (debate)
Video of the speeches
脗聽

脗聽脗聽Presidente. 芒聙聯 L'ordine del giorno reca la relazione di Matja脜戮 Nemec, a nome della commissione per le libert脙聽 civili, la giustizia e gli affari interni, sulla proposta di regolamento del Parlamento europeo e del Consiglio che modifica il regolamento (UE) 2018/1806 per quanto riguarda la revisione del meccanismo di sospensione (COM(2023)0642 - C9-0392/2023 - 2023/0371(COD) (A10-0035/2025).

脗聽
脗聽脗聽
脗聽

脗聽脗聽Matja脜戮 Nemec, rapporteur. 芒聙聯 Madam脗聽President,脗聽Commissioner Brunner, dear colleagues, tomorrow Parliament will vote on the reform of the visa suspension mechanism 芒聙聯 one of the key elements of the common EU visa policy.

In June this year, under the Polish Presidency, we reached an agreement on a compromise that will allow the temporary suspension of the EU visa-free regime with a third country under updated, flexible and more modern rules. Currently, the EU visa-free regime applies to 61 third countries worldwide and, until now, the temporary suspension of visa-free travel was only possible for limited reasons, mainly related to migration or security risks. While we have always defended a common visa policy, we have to reject attempts to use the suspension mechanism primarily as a migration management tool.

Nevertheless, an evident gap has existed between the preconditions for abolishing visas in the first place and, on the other hand, their temporary reintroduction when circumstances require it.

Dear colleagues, the world around us is more unstable than at any time since the Second World War. With more than 110 armed conflicts ongoing across the globe 芒聙聯 some at Europe's very borders 芒聙聯 we are facing new geopolitical realities and constantly emerging challenges. At the same time, Europe remains the most visited continent in the world by tourists and continues to receive a high volume of business travellers every year. The EU visa policy is therefore not only one of the key pillars of the Union's image in the world, but also one of its strongest foreign policy tools. A tool to promote European values abroad, it must serve as leverage as well. In times of global crisis, we must be able to respond collectively as a Union, so visa policy can no longer remain immune to these realities.

For the first time, thanks also to this Parliament, the EU will be able to suspend visa-free travel in cases of serious violation of international law or human rights. Furthermore, the Commission will have greater flexibility to target only specific categories of passports throughout the suspension period. When a foreign government seriously breaches international law and shows no respect for human rights, its government officials and diplomats should no longer enjoy visa-free access to Europe.

At the same time, the general population should not normally bear the consequences of the government's actions. But let me be clear. I will use the example of Israeli soldiers committing war crimes in Gaza and even boasting about it on social media 芒聙聯 it is against our values and immoral to welcome them with open arms in Europe as tourists.

Dear colleagues, with this agreement, we are finally modernising the visa suspension mechanism so that it reinforces our commitment to human rights and international law. The mechanism must serve as a tool to discourage third countries from undermining the international and humanitarian values that the European Union stands for. When the temporary suspension of visa-free travel with third countries is being considered, the European Commission will now also have to assess how this may affect civil society actors.

Finally, dear colleagues, let me thank all my colleagues for their support and the Polish Presidency for the excellent cooperation during the negotiations. I very much look forward to today's debate.

脗聽
脗聽脗聽
脗聽

脗聽脗聽Magnus Brunner, Member of the Commission. 芒聙聯 Madam President, good evening, everyone. Ladies and gentlemen, honourable Members, we heard from the rapporteur that it's actually a good thing to have visa-free regimes with 61 countries. And I would also like to thank all of you for the very good cooperation we had the last time when working on this topic.

In principle, of course, visa-free regimes are a win-win situation. They promote 芒聙聯 as we heard before 芒聙聯 tourism, economic developments, academic developments, academic exchanges. They boost trade. We heard all this. Innovation and technology haven't been mentioned, and of course also the European values.

But, of course, there are also migration and security challenges 芒聙聯 for example, when visa-free travellers overstay and submit unfounded asylum applications. A more modern visa suspension mechanism will exactly address these shortcomings. It also, in a way, responds to the geopolitical realities we face, with hybrid attacks in the East, and a weaponisation of migration also, which we feel almost every day.

The first principle, actually, of good visa-free policy is trust. We expect our visa-free partners to align their visa policies with those of the European Union. Otherwise they may become transit hubs, for instance, for irregular migration to the European Union, and that's something, of course, we cannot accept.

Similarly, we cannot accept partner countries running investor citizenship schemes. That's also something, of course, we have to be aware of 芒聙聯 schemes that pose a risk for our internal security. These schemes can allow infiltration of organised crime, money laundering, tax evasion and also corruption in the European Union. And being a partner of the European Union comes, of course, with benefits on the one hand, but on the other hand also with obligations.

From now on, we will have new grounds to suspend visa-free regimes. Firstly, when a third country operates investor citizenship schemes, as I just mentioned. Secondly, when the third country does not align its visa policy with the European Union in a sufficient way. And thirdly, when our external relations with them deteriorate 芒聙聯 in particular, of course, for serious breaches of human rights, as we just heard before, and in general, breaching international law.

These new grounds are in addition to those already in the legislation, including sudden or substantial increases in irregular migration, lack of readmission cooperation, or other security risks. And the updated visa suspension mechanism includes a stronger monitoring and a stronger reporting obligation. The Commission will report to the European Parliament and the Council on any visa-free country where we identify different challenges.

All in all, we are implementing a quicker, more flexible visa policy with similar objectives 芒聙聯 a mechanism that is better suited also for the times we are in. And, by the end of the year, we are also going to present our new visa policy strategy. That is also very important 芒聙聯 visa plays a very important role altogether when it comes to agreements with a third country, and it should actually contribute to the European Union's economic growth, to the competitiveness, strengthening also EU security and border management.

Altogether, I think a very good day for our policies, especially when it comes to our policies with third countries.

脗聽		脗聽

脗聽脗聽Nikola Bart脜炉脜隆ek (PfE), in writing. 芒聙聯 Voting in favor of the visa suspension mechanism is a crucial step towards strengthening the EU芒聙聶s external border management and ensuring the integrity of the Schengen area. This mechanism allows the European Union to temporarily suspend or restrict visa issuance for citizens of third countries if those countries fail to cooperate on key issues such as readmission of their nationals, security, or migration management. This mechanism acts as a diplomatic tool, encouraging third countries to engage constructively with the EU on migration and security cooperation. It creates an incentive for countries to fulfil their obligations, including the timely readmission of illegal migrants. This is what our voters want from us and this is what I am delivering to them. Right now, we have a problem in the EU, with failed asylum seekers disregarding the return decisions and staying in the EU. With the visa suspension mechanism we will be able to put pressure on the countries of origin to take back their citizens so that they do not stay in Europe against orders to leave.
